    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="c507df0f7dad4aefae85b6fe648e0a66">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '21df6507-fb84-4d2c-a2bb-d7f8f00037da'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d285927308" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>The coordination with Na<sup>+</sup> in electrolyte induces excessive dissolution and electric field driven delocalization of Na<sub>2</sub>S, causing solo oxidations of discharge products, which is revealed as the cycling failure mechanism for MS anode. Therefore, the coordination regulation of Na<sub>2</sub>S is proposed as an effective strategy.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/833bc4f0-8d11-4a0d-b1bb-ab01a69a9e4f/anie202403463-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/833bc4f0-8d11-4a0d-b1bb-ab01a69a9e4f/anie202403463-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/76897f59-7a5a-4b45-96b4-204809155cc4/anie202403463-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/833bc4f0-8d11-4a0d-b1bb-ab01a69a9e4f/anie202403463-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d285927311" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Metal sulfide (MS) is regarded as a promising candidate of the anode materials for sodium-ion battery (SIB) with ideal capacity and low cost, yet still suffers from the inferior cycling stability and voltage degradation. Herein, the coordination relationship between the discharge product Na<sub>2</sub>S with the Na<sup>+</sup> (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) in the electrolyte, is revealed as the root cause for the cycling failure of MS. Na<sup>+</sup>-coordination effect assistants the dissolution of Na<sub>2</sub>S, further delocalizing Na<sub>2</sub>S from the reaction interface under the function of electric field, which leads to the solo oxidation of the discharge product element metal without the participation of Na<sub>2</sub>S. Besides, the higher highest occupied molecular orbital of Na<sub>2</sub>S suggest the facilitated Na<sub>2</sub>S solo oxidation to produce sodium polysulfides (NaPSs). Based on these, lowering the Na<sup>+</sup> concentration of the electrolyte is proposed as a potential improvement strategy to change the coordination environment of Na<sub>2</sub>S, suppressing the side reactions of the solo-oxidation of element metal and Na<sub>2</sub>S. Consequently, the enhanced conversion reaction reversibility and prolonged cycle life are achieved. This work renders in-depth perception of failure mechanism and inspiration for realizing advanced conversion-type anode.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202403463-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202403463-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The continual increase in the demand for higher energy density in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) necessitates the development of novel anode and cathode materials. In particular, metal chalcogenides have been extensively investigated as a conversion-type anode material due to their higher specific capacity and lower cost.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0001_R_d285927299e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Storage Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#anie202403463-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0003_R_d285927299e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a></sup></span> Metal sulfides (MSs), in comparison to metal oxides and selenides, exhibit a favorable conversion reactivity and a greater gravimetric capacity, endowing them with attractive potential for applications in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0004_R_d285927299e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Eng. J.">4</a>, <a href="#anie202403463-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0005_R_d285927299e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>However, structural damage derived from volume expansion, slow charge transfer originated from semiconductivity, and the formation of soluble sodium polysulfides (NaPSs), lead to the deficient cycling reversibility of MS anodes, which hinders their development in SIBs. To address these issues, numerous studies have been conducted, such as morphology modulation, nanostructure engineering, phase engineering and integration with the conductive matrix.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0006_R_d285927299e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Eng. J.">6</a>-<a href="#anie202403463-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0010_R_d285927299e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span> In recent years, application of multiple metals as guests to realize heterostructure or solid solution phase,<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0011_R_d285927299e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Nano">11</a>-<a href="#anie202403463-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0016_R_d285927299e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span> along with SEI optimization realized by electrolyte modification,<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0017_R_d285927299e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Materials Lett.">17</a>-<a href="#anie202403463-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0019_R_d285927299e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a></sup></span> have also made great advance in inhibiting pulverization and boosting kinetics.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">However, the generation of soluble NaPSs remains the unsolved problem, and the main cause for the loss of metal sulfide active materials and current collector corrosion, which will finally result in the capacity decay and the electrode structure deterioration of SIBs. Thereby, the formation mechanisms of the NaPSs should be intensively investigated. In the reduction process (sodiation), the typical MS is reduced to the element metal (M) and Na<sub>2</sub>S, in which it is not possible to produce NaPSs. During the oxidation process (desodiation), with the participation of Na<sub>2</sub>S, the element M is oxidized to be the MS (Equation 1), which is supposed to be the predominant reaction. Given that NaPSs is known to be generated, the solo oxidation of Na<sub>2</sub>S without the involvement of the element M must have occurred (Equation 2). Although the absorption and catalysis strategies, drawing inspiration from Li−S and Na−S batteries, have been employed to reduce the shuttling and facilitate the redox for NaPSs,<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0020_R_d285927299e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Storage Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#anie202403463-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0021_R_d285927299e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span> the root cause for the NaPSs generation, namely, the solo oxidation of Na<sub>2</sub>S, and the electrochemical behavior of the remaining element M have rarely been studied.
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202403463-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2c637ac7-e5e8-4bb6-8707-6b7411a22865/anie202403463-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202403463-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/0aacbb2e-78d2-4160-8883-afd3a231aba3/anie202403463-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202403463-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a826235d-91b0-4e47-8325-9a72f18f3dd4/anie202403463-math-0003.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>Herein, the factors contributing to the production of NaPSs and deteriorating the cycling performance were elucidated by investigating the electrochemical behavior of MS, and the potential strategies for improvement were subsequently explored. It was revealed for the first time that the solubility of Na<sub>2</sub>S is positively correlated with the concentration of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in the electrolyte, due to a dissolving-assistance effect of Na<sup>+</sup>. Na<sup>+</sup> could coordinate with Na<sub>2</sub>S strongly, forming Na<sup>+</sup>-Na<sub>2</sub>S cluster and inducing the Na<sub>2</sub>S into the electrolyte. In the conventional electrolyte (1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in dimethoxyethane (DME)), the considerable Na<sup>+</sup> led to a large amount of Na<sub>2</sub>S dissolution, which were repelled by the interfacial electric field under the oxidation (desodiation) potential, resulting in the inadequate involvement of Na<sub>2</sub>S in conversion reaction (Equation 1). Besides, in the absence of Na<sub>2</sub>S, part of the element M was solitarily oxidized to the metal ions (M<sup>2+</sup>) into the electrolyte (Equation 3), resulting in the loss of the metal component. Also, the solo oxidation of Na<sub>2</sub>S was followed by the formation of NaPSs, which not only caused the loss of sulfur components, but also corroded the current collector and deteriorated the electrode structure. Based on these fundamentals, a novel strategy of lowering the NaPF<sub>6</sub> concentration (0.1 M) was proposed, which could suppress the dissolving assistance effect of Na<sup>+</sup> and re-involve the dissolved Na<sub>2</sub>S in conversion reaction of MS due to the mitigation of the electric repulsion. Meanwhile, solo Na<sub>2</sub>S oxidation is inhibited by the slower kinetics affected by solubility and the lower highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) induced by coordination environment variation in 0.1 M electrolyte. Therefore, the 0.1 M electrolyte achieved significantly improved cycling reversibility (412.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 84 % of the theoretical capacity, after 800 cycles) and notable maintenance of potential plateaus. This work provided new insights for the failure mechanisms of MS anode, as well as the feasible improvement strategies.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202403463-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202403463-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>From a thermodynamic perspective, a lower sodiation/desodiation potential could prioritize the main reaction (Equation 1), theoretically inhibiting the side reactions (Equation 2). After comprehensively assessing the sodiation potentials together with the cost of common metal sulfides, as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0001">1a</a>, ZnS with a comparatively lower sodiation potential and low cost was proposed as an ideal model anode material to investigate the electrochemical behavior of the MS anodes for SIBs. A low-cost organic salt zinc diethyldithiocarbamate was used to prepare a nitrogenous carbon coated ZnS composite through a facile single precursor decomposition method.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0022_R_d285927299e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A">22</a></sup></span> Series of physical characterizations were conducted to study the information of the prepared sample. The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns in Figure S1 shows that all the peaks can match well with the wurtzite ZnS phase (PDF#75-1547) without impurity signals. Field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) exhibit the irregular shape of ZnS@C with a diameter of 200–300 nm in Figures S2, S3a and S3b. Amorphous carbon layer with around 10 nm thickness is also observed in HRTEM (Figure S3c). The information provided by the selected area electronic diffraction (SAED) in Figure S3d is consistent with the XRD result (Figure S1). The uniform distribution of N and C in energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS) element mappings of Figure S3e suggests the homogeneous nitrogen doping in carbon layer, which is also proved by C−N peak located at 285.5 eV in the XPS C 1s spectrum (Figure S4a).<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0023_R_d285927299e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Nano">23</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure S4b–d, the element N is mainly existed in the pyrrolic and pyridinic form,<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0024_R_d285927299e865" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Eng. J.">24</a></sup></span> and the S 2p and Zn 2p spectra correspond to the characteristic peaks of ZnS.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0025_R_d285927299e868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a>, <a href="#anie202403463-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0026_R_d285927299e868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">26</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the Raman spectrum in Figure S5 exhibits the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio of 1.16, revealing a significant number of defects in the carbon layer attributed by N doping. And the absence of phonon peaks associated with ZnS is resulted from the integrated encapsulation of the carbon layer on ZnS nanoparticles. The content of carbon was calculated to be around 11.5 % by the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) in Figure S6. All these physical characterizations conclude that the nitrogen doped carbon layer coated ZnS has been successfully synthesized.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202403463-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fa3c97a1-806e-475d-aa2c-8644c59797b6/anie202403463-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fa3c97a1-806e-475d-aa2c-8644c59797b6/anie202403463-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/75354f75-e31b-4f86-ae1f-ae2fb4ba4438/anie202403463-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fa3c97a1-806e-475d-aa2c-8644c59797b6/anie202403463-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403463-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403463" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Calculated sodiation/desodiation equilibrium potentials of various MSs obtained from Gibbs formation energy (G<sub>f</sub>) with the metal material price comparisons. (b) CV curves of ZnS@C at the scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. (c) Discharge/charge profiles of 50<sup>th</sup> ~450<sup>th</sup> and the last (468<sup>th</sup>) cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (d) dQ/dV curves from 50<sup>th</sup> to 450<sup>th</sup> cycles. (e) UV/Vis spectra of the electrolyte with the disassembled separator (inset) after cycling. (f) SEM image of the electrode after 100 cycles and the corresponding EDS linear scanning. (g) Schematic diagram for the failure mechanism derived from Cu<sub>x</sub>S formation and NaPSs shuttling.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Half-cell of SIB was assembled with ZnS@C as the cathode material, metallic Na as anode material, and 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME as the electrolyte to evaluate the sodium storage performance of the prepared ZnS@C composite. As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0001">1b</a>, the reduction peaks at 0.34 V and 0.20 V displayed in the first cyclic voltammetry (CV) curve correspond to the conversion from ZnS to Zn<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>S, and the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). Subsequent CV cycles from 2nd to 5th embody a reduction peak lifted to 0.50 V and oxidation peak around 0.92 V after activation. The discharge/charge voltage plateaus at about 0.50 V and 0.90 V in voltage-capacity profiles, as shown in Figure S7a, are coincident to the CV curves. The initial capacities of the half cells at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> were 588 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 576 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (based on the total weight of ZnS@C), respectively. And the remaining capacities after 120 cycles were 550 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 540 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure S7b), with the coulombic efficiency (CE) of nearly 100 %.</p>
         
         <p>Discouragingly, reversible de/sodiation was not preserved well as the cycling test was extended to several hundred cycles. A serious capacity fluctuation becomes evident, especially from the 100th to the 450th cycles (Figure S8). During this period, the minimum capacity of 390 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is observed at the 340th cycle, followed by a capacity increase. Even more concerning is the potential evolution. The charge/discharge profiles and the dQ/dV curves from 50th to 450th cycle both exhibit an obnoxious upward shift of the voltage plateaus, which predicts the denaturation of the conversion reaction (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0001">1c</a> and <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0001">d</a>). Two additional oxidation peaks (1.51 V and 1.82 V) and several reduction peaks (three peaks in the range of 0.5–0.9 V, and one peak at 1.55 V) were presented from the 250th cycle, and the intensity of these peaks increase with the increment of the cycle number. Finally, the overcharge failure occurs at 468th cycle, announcing the end of cycle life.</p>
         
         <p>To clarify this reaction evolution, cell was disassembled after cycling to detect the ingredient of the electrolyte and the morphology of the electrode. Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0001">1e</a> shows the UV/Vis spectra of the pure DME solvent, the electrolyte after 5 cycles and that after failure, respectively. An evident UV absorption peak (320 nm) associated with NaPSs is detected in the electrolyte after failure.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0027_R_d285927299e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Small Methods">27</a></sup></span> In the inset, yellow plaques can be observed on the glass fiber separator, implying a substantial release of NaPSs into the electrolyte, which has been reported as a typical side reaction in the conversion reaction of MSs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0021_R_d285927299e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#anie202403463-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0027_R_d285927299e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Small Methods">27</a>, <a href="#anie202403463-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0028_R_d285927299e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a></sup></span> In the SEM image (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0001">1f</a>), newly emerging micro-flowers with diameter over 1 μm are observed on the electrode after 100 cycles, which can hardly be recognized as ZnS@C particles or species derived from the electrolyte. EDS linear scanning highlights a copper-enriched distribution in the position of these nanoflowers. Considering that NaPSs could react with Cu to form Cu<sub>x</sub>S, it is reasonable to infer that these micro-flowers are attributed to the formation of Cu<sub>x</sub>S from the side reaction of the Cu current collector corrosion.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0029_R_d285927299e975" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the elevated voltage plateaus in the charge and discharge profiles and dQ/dV curves coincide with the sodiation plateaus of Cu<sub>x</sub>S.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0030_R_d285927299e980" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano-Micro Lett.">30</a>, <a href="#anie202403463-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0031_R_d285927299e980" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Small Structures">31</a></sup></span> The high sodiation/desodiation plateaus of Cu<sub>x</sub>S imply less thermodynamic priority for conversion reaction compared to ZnS, leading to the tendency of side reaction to produce more NaPSs (Equation 2). This process reversely aggravates the corrosion of NaPSs on the Cu current collector again. As a consequent, a deteriorated evolution from ZnS to Cu<sub>x</sub>S is implemented and the overcharge failure is trigued by the NaPSs accumulation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0021_R_d285927299e988" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span> The failure mechanism is schematically illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0001">1g</a>.</p>
         
         <p>To further investigate the corrosive behavior of the parasitically formed NaPSs, a half cell using Al foil as current collector was also conducted in cycling test. As shown in Figure S9, only several cycles were conducted when the pure Al foil was used as the current collector for ZnS@C active materials. The improvements of cycling performance of ZnS@C based SIBs with porous carbon coating Al foil current collector confirm the corrosion of Al foil current collector by NaPSs during the discharge/charge process (Figure S10), since the adsorption of NaPSs on porous carbon can efficiently alleviate the corrosions. The SEM images of the Al current collector shows the corrosion of Al foil surface even though only several cycles of the SIB were conducted (Figure S11).</p>
         
         <p>It becomes evident that the release and shuttling of NaPSs are inescapable factors to significantly impair battery longevity and energy density. Therefore, solo oxidation of Na<sub>2</sub>S must be suppressed to improve the cycling performance. When examining both the conversion reaction (Equation 1) and the side reaction (Equation 2), it is intriguingly discovered that the solubility of Na<sub>2</sub>S may have a great influence on both reactions, since absolute solid/solid reaction is difficult to perform from a kinetic point of view. In other words, the solubility of Na<sub>2</sub>S in the electrolyte may determine the competitiveness between the conversion reaction of ZnS (Equation 1) and the side reaction for the generation of NaPSs (Equation 2).</p>
         
         <p>To explore this hypothesis, MD calculation was initially employed to investigate the solubility of Na<sub>2</sub>S in the conventional electrolyte. An electrolyte system containing 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME solvent with 0.2 M of Na<sub>2</sub>S was established to simulate the dispersion states of Na<sub>2</sub>S in electrolyte. In the snapshot (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2a</a>), Na<sub>2</sub>S was noted to be uniformly dispersed in the simulated electrolyte space, implying an unexpected high solubility of Na<sub>2</sub>S. Through the localized structure illustrated in Figure S12a, the dispersed Na<sub>2</sub>S is closely coordinated with several Na<sup>+</sup> in electrolyte and forms Na<sup>+</sup>-Na<sub>2</sub>S clusters. This coordination, which is due to the attraction between negatively charged S of Na<sub>2</sub>S and positively charged Na<sup>+</sup>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0032_R_d285927299e1037" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem">32</a></sup></span> is believed to realize an assistance effect for Na<sub>2</sub>S dissolution. NMR is further employed to test the 1.0 M electrolyte and that saturated with Na<sub>2</sub>S. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2b</a>, the saturation of Na<sub>2</sub>S shifts the peak of Na nucleus from −0.2 ppm to −0.7 ppm in 1.0 M electrolyte, corresponding to the shielding of the Na nucleus by more negatively charged S. Besides, PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> is also noticed to form coordination in the outer layer of Na<sup>+</sup>-Na<sub>2</sub>S cluster. It can be seen that Na<sub>2</sub>S is sufficiently attracted to the original ions in the electrolyte and presumably causes destructions to the pristine solvated structure of the electrolyte without Na<sub>2</sub>S. And that explains the reason why the H nucleus peaks shift towards the direction of pure DME after Na<sub>2</sub>S saturation (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2c</a>).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202403463-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/48c5b66e-51ae-4bf1-86dc-77177a18dcab/anie202403463-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/48c5b66e-51ae-4bf1-86dc-77177a18dcab/anie202403463-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cbb9330f-4b0f-4957-9a9f-15d6a191e673/anie202403463-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/48c5b66e-51ae-4bf1-86dc-77177a18dcab/anie202403463-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403463-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403463" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Distribution Snapshot of Na<sub>2</sub>S in (a) conventional (1.0 M) and (d) dilute (0.1 M) electrolyte by MD calculation. NMR results of (b, e) <sup>23</sup>Na and (c, f) <sup>1</sup>H spectra of the two electrolyte and them saturated with Na<sub>2</sub>S. (g–j) Intermolecular radial distribution functions (RDFs) and coordination number (CN(r)). (k) Schematic diagram of Na<sub>2</sub>S-saturated electrolyte and their optical photos. (l) Integral area ratio of <sup>23</sup>Na peak to <sup>1</sup>H peak.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Based on these observations, it was reasonable to believe that the Na<sup>+</sup> functions as an assistant for the Na<sub>2</sub>S dissolution, thus less Na<sup>+</sup> would reduce the dispersion of Na<sub>2</sub>S. Thus, another system of low concentration electrolyte (0.1 M) with identical amount of Na<sub>2</sub>S (0.2 M) was simulated. The result shows that, in the 0.1 M electrolyte, Na<sub>2</sub>S exists predominantly in the form of agglomerates as the consequence of inadequate Na<sup>+</sup>, which implies a lower solubility (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2d</a> and S12b). Correspondingly, the shift of Na peak to low-field (from −1.40 ppm to 0.54 ppm) after Na<sub>2</sub>S saturation is observed in NMR result (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2e</a>), suggesting the destruction of Na<sub>2</sub>S on solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup> outweighs the meagre shielding effect of Na<sub>2</sub>S itself on Na<sup>+</sup>. Besides, it is noticed from Figure S10d that the 2.67 Å distance between negatively charged S of Na<sub>2</sub>S and positively charged H of the DME solvent molecular in the 0.1 M electrolyte is shorter than the sum of the van der Waals radii of S and H atoms, reasonably suggesting the formation of a coordination structure between S and H like hydrogen bond. This interaction between S and H is also verified by the shift of the H peaks to high field (from 4.1–4.6 ppm to 3.7–4.1 ppm) (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2f</a>). And the emerging peaks at 4.04 ppm and 4.85 ppm which are highlighted by red pots once again support the formation of hydrogen bond.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0033_R_d285927299e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Sci.">33</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>This difference in solubility is further manifested in the Radial Distribution Function (RDF). Na<sub>2</sub>S in the 0.1 M electrolyte yields much higher value of g(r) for S−Na peak at coordination distance of 0.234 nm and S−S peak at 0.35 nm (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2g</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0034_R_d285927299e1156" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nano Lett.">34</a></sup></span> Also, a cleavage of S−S peak is noticed at 0.38 nm, where the coordination number (CN(r)) of S decreases from 3.1 in 0.1 M electrolyte to 1.35 in 1.0 M electrolyte (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2h</a>). These indicate that S and Na are more tightly aggregated in 0.1 M electrolyte as demonstrated by the snapshot of MD calculations. Furthermore, the concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> also contributes to the disparities in the coordination environment of S. Instinct g(r) peaks of S−C at 0.29 nm and S−H at 0.37 nm 0.1 M electrolyte illustrate more intimate interactions between Na<sub>2</sub>S and DME molecules than in the 1.0 M electrolyte (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2i</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0035_R_d285927299e1170" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">35</a></sup></span> Compared with 1.0 M electrolyte, 0.1 M electrolyte delivers higher CN(r) of H and C at any distance from S (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2j</a>), further confirming the more intimate interactions between Na<sub>2</sub>S and DME molecules.</p>
         
         <p>The saturation experiments also demonstrate the law of Na<sub>2</sub>S solubility dependent on the electrolyte concentration. Compared with the Na<sub>2</sub>S saturated 0.1 M electrolyte, the Na<sub>2</sub>S saturated 1.0 M electrolyte shows a darker color in the optical photographs (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2k</a>). And in the UV/Vis absorption test, the absorption peak corresponding to Na<sub>2</sub>S (wavelength of ~237 nm) reflects higher value in the Na<sub>2</sub>S saturated 1.0 M electrolyte (Figure S13), implying a higher solubility of Na<sub>2</sub>S in 1.0 M electrolyte than that in 0.1 M electrolyte. By contrasting the integral area ratios of the H- and Na-spectrum peaks(Table S4 and Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2l</a>), about 0.225 M Na<sub>2</sub>S was quantitatively calculated to be dissolved in 1.0 M electrolyte,<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0036_R_d285927299e1202" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">36</a></sup></span> while essentially no proportional change is observed at 0.1 M electrolyte, which verifies the low Na<sub>2</sub>S solubility in 0.1 M electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p>Based on foregoing analysis, the proposed dilute electrolyte that is expected to reduce the dissolution and solo oxidation of Na<sub>2</sub>S should be inhibited due to the suppressed kinetic of solid reaction comparing with liquid reaction.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0035_R_d285927299e1212" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">35</a>, <a href="#anie202403463-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0037_R_d285927299e1212" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a>, <a href="#anie202403463-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0038_R_d285927299e1212" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Eng. J.">38</a></sup></span> Therefore, the 0.1 M electrolyte was applied in the electrochemical evaluation of the ZnS@C electrode. In the CV curves (Figure S14), the oxidation and reduction peaks of ZnS@C electrode in 0.1 M electrolyte stabilize at 0.95 V and 0.44 V, respectively. The galvanostatic cycle test is shown in Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3a</a>. In the 1.0 M electrolyte, the ZnS@C electrode experienced overcharge failure at 468th cycles, under the current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Comparatively, ZnS@C in 0.1 M electrolyte displays a cycle life prolonged to 800 cycles, with a residual capacity of 412.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (84 % of the theoretical capacity of 486.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for ZnS@C with considering the content of carbon layer). In the charge/discharge curves and dQ/dV curves (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3b</a> and <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3c</a>), superior to 1.0 M electrolyte, it retains the voltage plateaus corresponding to the conversion reaction of ZnS within 450 cycles in 0.1 M electrolyte. This affirms that 0.1 M electrolyte can effectively inhibit the production of NaPSs, and thus promote cycling reversibility. In addition, the ZnS@C||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cells were assembled, employing Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as cathode material and ZnS@C as anode material, with N/P ratio of 1.3, in the 0.1 M electrolyte, which was tested for the electrochemical performance. It delivers a reversible capacity of 334 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, further validating the excellent reversibility assisted by 0.1 M electrolyte (Figure S15a and S15b).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202403463-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eeb02634-c626-4e07-a807-d7930bc3aa16/anie202403463-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/eeb02634-c626-4e07-a807-d7930bc3aa16/anie202403463-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ef2fcd42-00a1-4c5e-a0ee-ae7391560a09/anie202403463-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eeb02634-c626-4e07-a807-d7930bc3aa16/anie202403463-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403463-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403463" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Cycling performance of ZnS@C at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> in 1.0 M and 0.1 M electrolyte. (b) Discharge and charge profiles, and (c) corresponding dQ/dV curves from 50<sup>th</sup> to 450<sup>th</sup> cycles in 0.1 M electrolyte. (d) XPS of S 2p of ZnS@C electrodes after different cycles in 1.0 M and 0.1 M electrolyte. (e) TEM, (f) SEAD, (g, h) HRTEM, (i) STEM images and (j) EDS mapping of ZnS@C after 100 cycles in 1.0 M electrolyte. (k) TEM, (l) SEAD, (m, n) HRTEM, (o) STEM images and (p) EDS mapping of ZnS@C after 100 cycles in 0.1 M electrolyte.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>XPS was further applied to provide insight into the surface elemental information of electrodes after cycling. Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3d</a> and S16 respectively display the S 2p and Zn 2p spectra of fresh ZnS@C electrode and electrodes after 10 and 100 cycles in different electrolyte at fully charged state. As shown in S 2p spectra, both electrodes at 0.1 M and 1.0 M electrolyte dominantly display the peaks of Zn−S bond after 10 cycles, which is consistent with the fresh ZnS@C electrode. However, after the next 100 cycles, the electrode cycled in 1.0 M electrolyte exhibits a distinct peak of Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0039_R_d285927299e1292" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nat. Commun.">39</a></sup></span> indicating that a significant amount of Na<sub>2</sub>S has not participated in the conversion reaction of ZnS but been solely oxidized to NaPSs during desodiation. In contrast, the electrode cycled in 0.1 M electrolyte still maintains the Zn−S peaks. The Zn 2p spectra display a similar trend with the S 2p spectra. After 100 cycles in 1.0 M electrolyte, the Zn 2p spectra illustrates a peak shift towards the low energy direction, suggesting the existence of low-valence state Zn and the irreversibility of conversion reaction. But in 0.1 M electrolyte, neither of the Zn 2p1/2 or Zn 2p3/2 peaks deviate from the initial position, indicating ZnS@C achieve a better reaction reversibility and stability when cycled in 0.1 M electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p>The electrodes after 100 cycles were also characterized by TEM and STEM. The micro-morphology is fairly well preserved under both conditions (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3e</a> and <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3k</a>). But in the SEAD pattern of Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3f</a>, the diffraction spots corresponding to Cu<sub>x</sub>S are observed in 1.0 M electrolyte. The diffraction rings belonging to ZnS become extremely obscure, implying the significantly disordering of the ZnS phase, which is consistent with the HRTEM image (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3g</a>). On the surface of the particles, lattice fringes related to Na<sub>2</sub>S crystal planes of (4 0 0) appear in the HRTEM images (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3h</a>), and the STEM image in Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3i</a> exhibits the trace of lattice fringes of Cu<sub>9</sub>S<sub>5</sub> (crystal planes of (0 0 15) and (0 1 2)), further emphasizing the unfavorable reversibility of ZnS in 1.0 M electrolyte. After cycling in 0.1 M electrolyte, the SEAD pattern indicates that the ZnS is well reproduced and no heterogeneous phases are present (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3l</a>). ZnS lattice fringes of (102) and (002) crystal planes can still be found in the HRTEM images (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3m</a> and <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3n</a>) though illegible fine grains have dominated most region, and also atomic image of STEM clearly displays the crystal planes of ZnS (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3o</a>). By comparison, the electrode after 100 cycles in 0.1 M electrolyte still maintains the ZnS phase.</p>
         
         <p>The EDS mappings of ZnS@C electrode in 1.0 M electrolyte were used to reveal the elements distribution of the electrode after cycling (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3j</a>). The dispersion of S that should primarily be within the ZnS@C particles, is observed to be scattered throughout the field. This confirms sulfur loss from ZnS, which may be caused by the dissolution of Na<sub>2</sub>S and the generation of NaPSs. The same outward spreading can also be observed in the Zn distribution. It is believed to be originated from the stripping and deposition of metal Zn, under the circumstance of inadequate involvement of Na<sub>2</sub>S in conversion reaction. In contrast, the EDS mapping for the ZnS@C after cycled in 0.1 M electrolyte reveals that both Zn and S are predominantly confined within the particles (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3p</a>). This proves that the loss of both active material Zn and Na<sub>2</sub>S, as well as the side reaction of NaPSs formation, are effectively inhibited in the 0.1 M electrolyte. In addition, SEM images of current collector Cu foil after 100 cycles in 0.1 M electrolyte displays a surface closely resembling fresh Cu foil, while newly appearing particles can be found on the Cu foil surface in 1.0 M electrolyte (Figure S17). It further confirms that the 0.1 M electrolyte could suppress the corrosion of Cu foil current collector via reducing the formation of NaPSs.</p>
         
         <p>To further explicitly investigate the dissolution of Na<sub>2</sub>S during discharging and charging, in situ Raman measurements were carried out to detect the variations in electrolyte. In the ex situ Raman characterization (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0004">4a</a>), it is witnessed that the C−O ang C−H stretching mode of DME are present in the range below 1200 cm<sup>−1</sup> and above 1400 cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. However, after adding certain amount of NaPF<sub>6</sub>, the characteristic peaks representing pure DME solvent are absent, suggesting that the solvation of NaPF<sub>6</sub> affects the response of DME to Raman signal. And the same phenomenon can be observed in both the ZnS saturated 0.1 M and ZnS saturated 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte, indicating the addition of ZnS did not change this situation. After being soaked with Na<sub>2</sub>S, the noticeable peaks belonging to DME reappear, which is believed to be caused by the desolvation of DME derived from the coordination between Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sup>+</sup>. And it is in agreement with the MD simulation and the NMR results (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0002">2</a>). This phenomenon serves as a useful indicator for characterizing the presence of dissolved Na<sub>2</sub>S in electrolyte.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202403463-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/09167a1a-db77-499b-8fcc-8144b213bd64/anie202403463-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/09167a1a-db77-499b-8fcc-8144b213bd64/anie202403463-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/55032802-a254-4665-b987-1006cbdbc025/anie202403463-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/09167a1a-db77-499b-8fcc-8144b213bd64/anie202403463-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403463-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403463" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Ex situ Raman spectra of different electrolytes. In situ Raman of (b) 0.1 M and (c) 1.0 M electrolyte with inset pictures of spectra at different states. MD calculations of Na<sub>2</sub>S in (d, e) 0.1 M and (f, g) 1.0 M electrolyte with electric field applied on electrolyte/solid interface.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In situ Raman spectra to detect the electrolyte on the surface of ZnS@C electrode during discharging/charging are shown in Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0004">4b</a> and <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0004">c</a>, respectively for 0.1 M and 1.0 M electrolyte. With the proceeding of discharge, the peaks representing DME in both electrolytes gradually appear, corresponding to the formation and the dissolution of Na<sub>2</sub>S in electrolyte. When charging upon around 2.5 V, the peaks assigned to DME in 0.1 M electrolyte disappear (as indicated by the dotted box in Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0004">4b</a>), indicating that the dissolved Na<sub>2</sub>S is consumed. In contrast, the peaks in 1.0 M electrolyte maintain a high intensity during the whole charging process. Even when the voltage exceeds 2.5 V, these peaks do not disappear (the right inset Raman pattern of Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0004">4c</a>), indicating that a large amount of dissolved Na<sub>2</sub>S still exist in the electrolyte at the end of charging, which does not participate in the conversion reaction of Zn to ZnS. In addition, the peak of NaPSs was not detected in in situ Raman spectra since NaPSs was not massively released in the first cycle (Figure S18).</p>
         
         <p>To explore the reason for the disparity in the in situ Raman during charging, the distributions of Na<sub>2</sub>S in 1.0 M and 0.1 M electrolyte at the electrolyte/electrode interface under the function of an electric field, are simulated by MD calculations. As illustrated in Figure S19a, the calculation results of substance distribution along Z-axis direction show that in 1.0 M electrolyte, PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> ions dominate the space close to the interface due to the electric field force. And the dissolved Na<sub>2</sub>S in 1.0 M electrolyte is distributed uniformly throughout the simulated space as demonstrated in Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0004">4d</a> and <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0004">4e</a>, which is the spatial delocalization of Na<sup>+</sup>-coordinated Na<sub>2</sub>S cation (Na<sup>+</sup>-Na<sub>2</sub>S) under electric field. Comparatively, for lack of Na<sup>+</sup> in 0.1 M electrolyte with even part of Na<sub>2</sub>S molecules coordinating with H of DME, it is witnessed that Na<sub>2</sub>S mainly distributes at the interface impelled by the electric field force (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0004">4f</a>, 4 g and S19b). Besides, the simulation without electric field shows that no substance, including Na<sub>2</sub>S and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, is attracted to the interface (Figure S20), further confirming the effect of electric field on the substance distribution. And this unique distribution of Na<sub>2</sub>S is conducive to its participation in the conversion reaction from Zn to ZnS.</p>
         
         <p>Different coordination relationships lead to different distribution states of Na<sub>2</sub>S, which will further affect the electrochemical behavior of metal Zn. Thus, a Zn foil oxidization experiment is conducted in different electrolytes to study the difference in the oxidation of Zn. The electrochemical setup, as illustrated in Figure S21a, applies Zn foil and Na foil as the working electrode (positive electrode) and the counter electrode (negative electrode) respectively, Na<sub>2</sub>S powder wrapped by double glass fiber separators is located between the Zn foil and the Na foil to keep the electrolyte saturated with Na<sub>2</sub>S and isolate Na<sub>2</sub>S at same time. As shown in Figure S21b, under the oxidation current, the three blue curves, which represents the Zn foil in 0.1 M, 1.0 M and Na<sub>2</sub>S-saturated 1.0 M electrolyte, exhibit voltage plateaus at approximately 2.5 V (Vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and visible etchings present in the SEM images (Figure S22a, S22b and S22c), thus it can be inferred that the anodic dissolution of metal Zn occurs, that is, Zn is oxidized into Zn<sup>2+</sup>. And the evidence of Na<sub>2</sub>S oxidation, Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>, is also noticed in the XPS S 2p spectra(Figure S21c). However, Zn foil in Na<sub>2</sub>S-saturated 0.1 M electrolyte exhibits a much smaller capacity above 2.5 V and polarizes severely to reach the cut-off voltage. Combined with the disappearance of etchings in SEM images and the characteristic signal of ZnS in XPS S 2p spectra (Figure S21c and S22d), it is confirmed that Na<sub>2</sub>S in 0.1 M electrolyte can be more involved in the oxidation process of Zn and generate ZnS to suppress the releasing of Zn<sup>2+</sup>, thus reducing the Zn loss which is also verified by the ICP results (Table S5).</p>
         
         <p>According to the results of in situ Raman, MD calculation and Zn oxidation experiments, it is concluded in Figure S21d that the strong delocalization effect of Na<sup>+</sup>-Na<sub>2</sub>S in the 1.0 M electrolyte induced by the electric field, results in the solo oxidation of Zn and Na<sub>2</sub>S, respectively (Equation 2 and 3). Due to the change of coordination environment, the mitigated delocalization effect in the 0.1 M electrolyte makes the dissolved Na<sub>2</sub>S mostly concentrated near the reaction interface, which is more conducive to participating in the main reaction as shown in Equation 1.</p>
         
         <p>Considering that the dissolution of Na<sub>2</sub>S may affect the electrochemical behavior on electrode surface, in situ EIS was used to investigate the variation in the oxidation reaction of Na<sub>2</sub>S caused by electrolyte concentration changing. In 1.0 M electrolyte, the charge transfer resistance of ZnS@C electrode always exhibits a single semicircle which corresponds to the conversion reaction of ZnS (R<sub>ZnS</sub>) at different charging and discharging potentials (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0005">5a</a>). However, in 0.1 M electrolyte, part of in situ EISs show the appearance of a second semicircle in the mid-frequency region, as displayed in the grey dotted wireframe of Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0005">5b</a>. It should be noticed that these emerging mid-frequency semicircles are observed only within the range after discharging to 0.4 V and before charging to 1.2 V, which is consistent with the redox potential range where solid Na<sub>2</sub>S should be generated on the electrode. And in the variable-temperature EIS tests, the emerging impedance on the ZnS@C electrode after discharging to 0.01 V demonstrates an abnormal positive temperature correlation in the 0.1 M electrolyte (Figure S23). Similarly, the R<sub>Na2S</sub> of the Na<sub>2</sub>S electrode, which corresponds to the redox of NaPSs/Na<sub>2</sub>S, also exhibits a distinct positive temperature correlation in the 0.1 M electrolyte (Figure S24a and S24b). It is therefore speculated that this emerging impedance in 0.1 M electrolyte corresponds to the redox of NaPSs/Na<sub>2</sub>S.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0037_R_d285927299e1545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a></sup></span> Further, the ZnS@C electrodes were fully discharged respectively in 1.0 M (denoted as 1.0 M) and 0.1 M (denoted as 0.1 M) electrolytes, and subsequently swapping their electrolytes to 0.1 M (denoted as 0.1 M′) and 1.0 M (denoted as 1.0 M′), the corresponding EIS are given in Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0003">3c</a>. It is found that the emerging impedance is always present in the 0.1 M electrolyte and is independent of the SEI discrepancy (Figure S16b and S16c). Since the electrode is at the discharge state, the EIS spectra must reflect the corresponding redox impedance of the discharge products metal Zn and Na<sub>2</sub>S. Combining the in situ EIS results and the EIS results for the electrolyte exchange experiment, 1.0 M electrolyte only display the R<sub>ZnS</sub>, whereas 0.1 M electrolyte delivers both R<sub>Na2S</sub> and R<sub>ZnS</sub>. This significantly increased R<sub>Na2S</sub> value, which is too small to be noticed in 1.0 M electrolyte, confirms that the kinetics of the side reaction of Na<sub>2</sub>S solo oxidation are effectively suppressed in 0.1 M electrolyte.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202403463-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f8814a23-a9ed-4ccb-9ab5-92637df8ea3e/anie202403463-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f8814a23-a9ed-4ccb-9ab5-92637df8ea3e/anie202403463-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/01606d80-1de0-498c-ac00-a63b57e67d77/anie202403463-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f8814a23-a9ed-4ccb-9ab5-92637df8ea3e/anie202403463-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403463-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403463" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>In situ EIS of ZnS@C during discharge and charge process in (a) 1.0 M, and (b) 0.1 M electrolyte. (c) Ex situ EIS of electrodes after discharging to 0.01 V in different electrolyte and the EIS of them with electrolyte exchange. (d) Frontier molecular orbitals of Na<sub>2</sub>S in 0.1 M and 1.0 M electrolyte. (e) Schematic diagram of Na<sub>2</sub>S properties in 0.1 M and 1.0 M electrolyte.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further investigate the effect of chemical environment on the solo Na<sub>2</sub>S oxidation tendency, two structures of Na<sub>2</sub>S were extracted from MD calculations and optimized by density functional theory (DFT) to analyze the frontier molecular orbitals in different environments. The selected structures are separately from Figure S12c and S12d, in which S atoms have different distance to H (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0005">5d</a> and S25). The lower HOMO level of Na<sub>2</sub>S in the 0.1 M electrolyte weakens the tendency of Na<sub>2</sub>S oxidation, which theoretically confirms the above experimental results (Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0005">5a</a>, <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0005">b</a> and <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0005">c</a>).</p>
         
         <p>XPS was then conducted on the ZnS@C electrodes after different discharging and charging processes in different electrolytes. The four distinct cases of (1) D1.0/C1.0, (2) D0.1/C1.0, (3) D0.1/C0.1, and (4) D1.0/C0.1 respectively represent that the electrodes were (1) discharged and charged in 1.0 M electrolyte, (2) discharged in 0.1 M electrolyte and charged in 1.0 M electrolyte, (3) discharged and charged in 0.1 M electrolyte, and (4) discharged in 1.0 M electrolyte and charged in 0.1 M electrolyte. It is evident in the S 2p spectra of Figure S26 that D0.1/C0.1 and D1.0/C0.1 exhibit closer match to the characteristic peaks of S 2p1/2 (161.5 eV) and S 2p3/2(162.7 eV) of ZnS, while D1.0/C1.0 and D0.1/C1.0 exhibit clear peak shift towards high energy, which is ascribed to the presence of sulfur species with higher valence state, such as Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub> or NaPSs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403463-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0030_R_d285927299e1623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano-Micro Lett.">30</a>, <a href="#anie202403463-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0040_R_d285927299e1623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a>, <a href="#anie202403463-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403463-bib-0041_R_d285927299e1623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">41</a></sup></span> This observations conclude that charging in 0.1 M electrolyte always results in a better reversibility of ZnS, regardless of the electrolyte in which the discharging step occurs.</p>
         
         <p>Besides, NaPSs diffusion experiment was carried out as shown in Figure S27. The rapid NaPSs diffusion in 0.1 M electrolyte suggests that the performance improvement in the 0.1 M electrolyte should be ascribed to suppression of Na<sub>2</sub>S dissolution and oxidation rather than the inhibition of the NaPSs diffusion.</p>
         
         <p>Overall, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202403463-fig-0005">5e</a>, Na<sub>2</sub>S, as the reactant of the desodiation, is featured as more soluble, and more easily to be delocalized and oxidized in 1.0 M electrolyte, thus exacerbating the irreversibility of conversion reaction and the occurrence of side reaction that produces Zn<sup>2+</sup> and NaPSs. In 0.1 M electrolyte, on the other hand, Na<sub>2</sub>S has much less solubility and less tendency to oxidize, and the less-delocalized Na<sub>2</sub>S can still be reintroduced into the electrode reaction at the oxidation potential. Together, these unique properties enable the 0.1 M electrolyte to express a better cyclic reversibility.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202403463-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202403463-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this study, ZnS with low sodiation/desodiation potential was chosen to investigate the cycling failure mechanism of the conversion-type MS anode. Based on the evolution of potential plateaus during cycling and the characterization of disassembled electrode after cycling, it was inferred that the generation of NaPSs leading to overcharge failure relates to the dissolution of Na<sub>2</sub>S. With the help of MD calculation and NMR test, the dissolution of Na<sub>2</sub>S originates from its strong coordination relationship with Na<sup>+</sup> in the conventional electrolyte (1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>). The formed Na<sup>+</sup>-Na<sub>2</sub>S cluster in 1.0 M electrolyte was confirmed by the in situ Raman and MD simulation to delocalize the Na<sub>2</sub>S from the reaction interphase under the electric field repulsion, reducing its participation in the main conversion reaction of ZnS, which further leads to solo oxidation of Zn to Zn<sup>2+</sup>. Meanwhile, it is revealed by the in situ EIS and DFT that the solubility and coordination environment of Na<sub>2</sub>S promotes the solo Na<sub>2</sub>S oxidation that produce NaPSs. According to the revealed failure mechanism, a strategy of lowering the concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolyte is proposed, which significantly suppresses all the degradation by changing the coordination environment of dissolved Na<sub>2</sub>S. Therefore, compared with conventional electrolyte, 0.1 M electrolyte delivered an improved maintenance of ZnS conversion reaction as well as a longer cycle life (412.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 84 % of the theoretical capacity, after 800 cycles). This study provides a novel perspective revealing the mechanism of sodium storage failure in metal sulfides, while simultaneously proposing a regulation strategy of electrolyte concentration to suppress the side reaction, which lays a solid foundation for the future application of metal sulfide anodes in lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
